        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.201802694')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="56784de0f6884a9a9e4ed5ad7538c700"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="56784de0f6884a9a9e4ed5ad7538c700">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1d723f4b-f6dd-4d03-b8cc-b607ee123048'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '99AF050A5E32B2F9E47F5E1A723C6875';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d145790710" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon is considered as one of the most promising anodes in sodium-ion batteries due to its high capacity, low cost, and abundant resources. However, the available capacity and low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) limits the practical application of hard carbon anode. This issue results from the unclear understanding of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism in hard carbon. In this work, a series of hard carbons with different microstructures are synthesized through an “up to down” approach by using a simple ball-milling method to illustrate the sodium-ion storage mechanism. The results demonstrate that ball-milled hard carbon with more defects and smaller microcrystalline size shows less low-potential-plateau capacity and lower ICE, which provides further evidence to the “adsorption–insertion” mechanism. This work might give a new perspective to design hard carbon material with a proper structure for efficient sodium-ion storage to develop high-performance sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802694-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802694-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries are considered as a potential solution for sustainable and cost-effective large-scale electric energy storage, because of its abundant sodium resources, low cost, and easy accessibility compared with lithium-ion batteries counterpart.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0001_R_d145790702e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater. Electrochem. Energy Rev.">1</a></span> However, it is a big challenge to find appropriate electrode materials to enhance the electrochemical performance of sodium-ion batteries due to its sluggish kinetic problem during the cycling process.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0002_R_d145790702e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">2</a></span> Therefore, many efforts have been made to gain a better electrochemical performance for room-temperature sodium-ion batteries involving both cathodes and anodes.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a></span> Recently, a number of oxides, ferrocyanide, and polyanionic compounds exhibit fairly good sodium storage capacity and cycling performance.[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a>, <a href="#smll201802694-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0004_R_d145790702e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chemistry">4</a></span>] Meanwhile, many kinds of anode materials show considerable capacity and stable cycling performance, such as carbonaceous materials,[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a></span>] alloy,[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a></span>] organic materials,[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a></span>] etc.</p>
         
         <p>Among all of these anode materials, carbon, especially hard carbon with turbostratic layer-stacked graphitic-like nanodomain and nanovoids, is considered to be a kind of promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries due to low potential plateau, high capacity (≈300 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>), and abundant resources.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0005_R_d145790702e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater. Sci. Bull.">5</a></span> Ding et al. exhibited peat moss derived hard carbon materials with a reversible capacity of 306 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and stable cycling performance.[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a></span>] Luo et al. found bleached pulp derived hard carbon nanofibers with a reversible capacity of 262.9 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with a stable cycling capacity of 176 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> over 600 cycles.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0006_R_d145790702e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">6</a></span> Alcantara et al. discovered resorcinol-formaldehyde derived hard carbon materials with microsphere structure and a reversible capacity of 285 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0007_R_d145790702e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Electrochem. Solid-State Lett.">7</a></span> Li et al. found out anthracite derived hard carbon materials with a reversible capacity of 222 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and a high initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 81%.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0008_R_d145790702e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a></span> These hard carbon materials exhibit various electrochemical performance due to their different microstructure. However, the intrinsic relationship between the electrochemical performance and the microstructure of hard carbon materials has remained unclear yet.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0009_R_d145790702e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater. Small">9</a></span> Generally, during sodiation and desodiation process, two obvious voltage regions for hard carbon electrode have been discovered: a slope voltage region between 1.0 V and 0.1 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and a low-voltage plateau voltage region between 0.1 and 0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), respectively. It is different from the sodiation–desodiation curves of graphite and soft carbon, which only show a slop-voltage region.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0010_R_d145790702e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Electrochem. Soc. Sci. Sin.: Chim.">10</a></span> To figure out the inner relationship between them, it is necessary to discover the sodium-ion storage mechanism in hard carbon, which is very important for the preparation of high-performance hard carbon anodes (high ICE, capacity, and cycling stability).</p>
         
         <p>Though many efforts have been made to explore the sodium-ion storage mechanism in hard carbon anode, it is still controversy and unclear yet. Dahn's group first proposed the “house of cards” structural model for hard carbon materials and the corresponding sodium storage mechanism in 2001: the slope-voltage region is mainly related to the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into graphene layer while the low-plateau voltage region is attributed to nanopore filling/plating of metallic sodium.[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0010_R_d145790702e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Electrochem. Soc. Sci. Sin.: Chim.">10</a></span>] Afterward, Komaba's group and Tarascon's group demonstrated this mechanism (called as “intercalation–adsorption” mechanism<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0011_R_d145790702e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></span>) through a series of experimental methods, like ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD), ex situ small-angle X-ray scattering (ex situ SAXS), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), etc.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0012_R_d145790702e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></span></p>
         
         <p>However, there are still some experimental phenomena cannot be explained by the “intercalation–adsorption” mechanism. For example, with increasing the pyrolyzed temperature, hard carbon would have fewer defects and nanopores, which means that it ought to exhibit less plateau capacity in the low potential according to the “intercalation–adsorption” mechanism. However, hard carbon pyrolyzed in a high temperature exhibited higher low-voltage-plateau capacity than that of hard carbon pyrolyzed in low temperature (below 1000 °C).[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a>, <a href="#smll201802694-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0013_R_d145790702e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano">13</a></span>] The experimental phenomenon is inconsistent with the “intercalation–adsorption” mechanism. In 2012, our group gave a new glimpse of the sodium-ion storage mechanism in hard carbon.[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a></span>] We proposed a “adsorption–insertion” mechanism, which was suggested that the low-voltage plateau capacity contributed to the intercalation into the graphene layer while the slope capacity mainly contributed to the absorption of sodium ions on the defects and active sites of the electrode surface. The theoretical calculation also showed that the appropriate interlayer distance of hard carbon (about 0.37 nm) is an important factor for sodium intercalation in carbonate electrolyte. Afterward, the mechanism was further demonstrated through a combination of in situ XRD, electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR), ex situ nuclear magnetic resonance (ex situ NMR), and simulations,<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0011_R_d145790702e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></span> which illustrated that the “adsorption–insertion” mechanism is in well match with its experimental results. According to the “adsorption–insertion” mechanism, hard carbon with low defect and low surface area by controlling pyrolysis rate was prepared, which can deliver a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 86.1% and high reversible capacity.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0014_R_d145790702e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></span> Mitlin's group synthesized a series of peat moss derived carbon materials and used ex situ XRD to discover the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> spacing expansion during discharge process between 0.1 and 0.001 V, which agreed with this mechanism.[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a></span>] Several groups also found the experimental results in accordance with the mechanism in some kind of way.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0015_R_d145790702e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Lett. Nano Energy Chem. Commun.">15</a></span> Actually, the different understanding towards sodium-ion storage mechanism might relate to complex structure in hard carbon. For example, the low crystallinity of hard carbon is related to the weak diffraction peaks of XRD, leading to difficulty in observing the structural change. Moreover, the precursor of hard carbon, doping elements and synthesizing condition also seriously affect the microstructure of the material, leading to the trouble in fully understanding the sodium-ion storage mechanism.</p>
         
         <p>The usual way to illustrate the sodium-ion mechanism is to prepare a series of hard carbon materials and then investigate the relation of their structure and performance.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0011_R_d145790702e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#smll201802694-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0014_R_d145790702e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#smll201802694-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0015_R_d145790702e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Lett. Nano Energy Chem. Commun.">15</a></span> Here, we exhibited an inverse approach to tune microstructure of the hard carbon by directly ball-milling hard carbon. We chose cellulose as a precursor to synthesis hard carbon, and then ball-milled as-prepared hard carbon materials to create different microcrystalline structure. The results demonstrated that more defects, more disorder structure and smaller microcrystalline size are related to poor ICE and less plateau capacity, which further confirmed the “adsorption–insertion” mechanism. This understanding might give a crucial sight to synthesis high-performance hard carbon materials for practical sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802694-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802694-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The morphologies of hard carbon materials are illustrated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0001">1</a>, the pyrolyzed hard carbon (HC) without further treatment appeared as a fiber-like shape with a rough surface. When increasing the ball-milling time, the particle size becomes smaller and some ball-milled fragments can be clearly observed. The SEM images indicate that the macroscopic morphology of the particles has changed after ball milling. In order to further discover the microstructural change during ball milling, the structural characterizations of the HCs were carried out. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0002">2</a>a showed the XRD patterns of the HC samples. The average interlayer <i>d</i>-spacing could be conducted according to Bragg's equation
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802694-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/9d9730f1-60e5-4114-b1b6-c010d158858e/smll201802694-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802694:smll201802694-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802694:smll201802694-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>d</i> is the average interlayer spacing, 2θ is the diffraction angle, λ is the wavelength of the incident X-ray excitation beam, <i>n</i> is the order of reflection.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0016_R_d145790702e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Proc. R. Soc. A﻿﻿">16</a></span> In Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0002">2</a>a, the position of the (002) peak of different HC samples almost does not change, indicating that all HC samples have similar <i>d</i>-spacing distance (0.375 nm) and short-range order structure. The average width (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>) and the thickness (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>) of the order-layered nanodomains could also be conducted by the XRD patterns according to the Debye–Scherrer equation<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0017_R_d145790702e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nachr. Ges. Wiss. Göttingen">17</a></span><div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802694-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/98b42737-a85b-4020-a5ca-aabb545366db/smll201802694-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802694:smll201802694-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802694:smll201802694-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802694-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/36b12130-9f5a-439c-a1d5-c75550c80d96/smll201802694-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/36b12130-9f5a-439c-a1d5-c75550c80d96/smll201802694-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5d7233f2-0a29-472f-8f29-bbdf7ceecde7/smll201802694-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/36b12130-9f5a-439c-a1d5-c75550c80d96/smll201802694-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802694-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802694" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of the HC, HC-1h, HC-2h, and HC-4h. Insets are magnified images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802694-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6f75b86a-8795-420b-b98f-8d2bd844b135/smll201802694-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6f75b86a-8795-420b-b98f-8d2bd844b135/smll201802694-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6dc09cda-cb97-417b-8ab9-54977c94236f/smll201802694-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6f75b86a-8795-420b-b98f-8d2bd844b135/smll201802694-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802694-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802694" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterization of the HCs. a) XRD patterns of the HCs, b) Raman spectra of the HCs, c) XPS spectra of the HCs, and d) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption curves of the HCs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">In this equation, <i>K</i> is the Scherrer constant (usually set as 0.89), γ is the wavelength of the X-ray beam, <i>B</i> is the full width at half maximum (FWHM) value of the diffraction peak, 2θ is the diffraction angle, and <i>L</i> is the average thickness in the perpendicular direction of the crystal plane.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0017_R_d145790702e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nachr. Ges. Wiss. Göttingen">17</a></span> The average thickness (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>) were determined by using the FWHM of the (002) peak (2θ ≈ 23°), exhibiting 1.13, 1.08, 1.07, and 1.03 nm for the HC, HC-1h, HC-2h, and HC-4h, respectively. Simultaneously, the average width of the order-layered nanodomains (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>) was calculated by using the FWHM of the (100) peak (2θ ≈ 43°), showing 2.82, 2.68, 2.50, and 2.07 nm for the HC, HC-1h, HC-2h, and HC-4h, respectively. And the number of the stacked graphene layers (N) decrease from 3.01 for the HC to 2.74 for the HC-4h, which is calculated by Equation <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll201802694-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a><div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802694-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/6a45f241-acb0-439d-aa9a-cb8b1a46b4ef/smll201802694-math-0003.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802694:smll201802694-math-0003" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802694:smll201802694-math-0003" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Both the average thickness and width of the order-layered nanodomains gradually decrease with the increase of ball-milling time, indicating that the size of order-layered nanodomains decrease.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Raman spectrum is also a good method to estimate the structural order by conducting the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio and the order-layered nanodomain size in the <i>a</i> direction (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>), which is displayed in Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0002">2</a>b. The G-band, located in about 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup> (<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>), is related to the relative motion of sp2 carbon atoms in both rings and chains.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0018_R_d145790702e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Phys. Rev. B﻿﻿">18</a></span> Another peak at about 1350 cm<sup>−1</sup> (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>), which is called the D-band, usually related to the degree of the disordered graphitic domains, is due to the breathing motion of the sp2 carbon atoms in rings located at the defects and the edge plane.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0018_R_d145790702e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Phys. Rev. B﻿﻿">18</a></span> Therefore, the intensity ratio of the D-band and G-band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) increases with increasing disorder degree of the carbon materials.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0018_R_d145790702e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Phys. Rev. B﻿﻿">18</a></span> <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> increases from 1.35 for HC to 1.64 for HC-4h, which shows the increase in defect and the decrease in the amount of order-layered nanodomains in carbon materials. The average width of the graphitic domains (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>) can also be calculated by the Tuinstra–Koenig formulation as Equation <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll201802694-disp-0004" title="Link to equation">4</a><div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802694-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/85b4bf92-ca7a-4853-a662-2cccfe26bed2/smll201802694-math-0004.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802694:smll201802694-math-0004" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802694:smll201802694-math-0004" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>where <i>C</i> is a parameter that relates to the laser wavelength, <i>R</i><sub>DG</sub> is the intensity ratio of the D-band and G-band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>).<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0019_R_d145790702e699" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Chem. Phys. Phys. Rev. B﻿﻿">19</a></span> With the above equation, <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> value decreases from 3.26 nm for HC to 2.68 nm for HC-4h. Though most of the <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> values calculated by the Raman spectra are higher than those calculated by the XRD patterns (Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0002">2</a>a), which is possibly due to the effect of small crystallites,<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0020_R_d145790702e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. J. Appl. Phys.">20</a></span> the reduced trend in <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> value is consistent with the XRD results.</div>
         
         <p>The C1s XPS is also an approach to analyze ordering degree of carbon layer, according to the variations of C(sp3) and C(sp2) ratio with different ball-milling time. Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0002">2</a>c showed XPS spectra of the different HC samples. The carbon 1s signal is deconvoluted into peaks at 284.5, 285.2, 285.9, and 286.7 eV (binding energy) and attributed to sp2, sp3, C<span class="icomoon"></span>O, and CO, respectively.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0021_R_d145790702e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nat. Nanotechnol.">21</a></span> Through fitting and calculating, the ratio of the sp2 C drops from 57.9% to 52.6%, while the ratio of sp3 C increases from 17.4% to 22.7% with the increase of ball-milling time. It demonstrates the partial destruction of the orderly carbon structure, which is in accordance with the above Raman results. The specific surface area and pore distribution were carried out by the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption curves shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0002">2</a>d. The HC-4h exhibits higher absorbed quantity compared with the other three hard carbon specimens both in the low-pressure region (P/P0 &lt; 0.35) and the high-pressure region (P/P0 ∼ 1) apparently. It indicates that the HC-4h has higher specific surface area and pore volume than the three others. The calculated specific surface area increases from 1.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for HC to 28.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for HC-4h. The pores of all the hard carbon materials almost distributed around 0.5 nm (Figure S2, Supporting Information). The results exhibit that hard carbon materials after ball milling have a larger specific surface area, but have similar pore distributions.</p>
         
         <p>The initial galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the hard carbon electrodes at a current rate of 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> were illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0003">3</a>a. The initial discharge/charge capacities of the HC, HC-1h, HC-2h, and HC-4h were 391.3/306.4, 380.9/193.4, 387.1/154.5, and 388.7/132 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. It is easy to be found that the initial discharge capacities remain almost same, but the ICE decreases from 78.3% for HC to 34% for HC-4h with increasing ball-milling time. The charge–discharge curves could be split into two parts: the low-voltage plateau region and the slop-voltage region. To further distinguish the trend of the different capacity, Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0003">3</a>b summarizes the variation of the plateau and the slope capacity of the different hard carbon materials in the initial charging and discharging process. In the discharge process, the plateau capacity severely decreases from 213 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for HC to 46.4 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for HC-4h, while the slope capacity sharply increases from the 178.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for HC to 342.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for HC-4h. In the charging process, the plateau capacity dramatically decreases with ball-milling time (181.8 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for the HC, 77.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for the HC-1h, 51.6 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for the HC-2h, 36.4 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for the HC-4h), while the slope capacity almost stays the same. It is obvious that the main loss of the charge capacity is due to the decrease of the plateau capacity. Associated with the mechanism (micropore filling of Na metal) proposed by Dahn's group,[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0010_R_d145790702e767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Electrochem. Soc. Sci. Sin.: Chim.">10</a></span>] this trend would be unexpected due to larger pore volume for the hard carbon with longer ball-milling time. However, according to the “adsorption–intercalation” mechanism,[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a></span>] this phenomenon is reasonable, which can be explained that the hard carbon with longer ball-milling time has more disorder structures and defects verified by Raman and XPS experiments (Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0002">2</a>b,c), leading to less sites of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in order-layered structure. The “adsorption–intercalation” mechanism also can be easy to explain the increase of the slope capacity in the initial discharging process, which is due to the presence of a lot of defects in ball-milled hard carbon materials, causing large irreversible capacity and low ICE (Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0003">3</a>b). The samples ball-milled under Ar were also tested to demonstrate the effects of oxygen. The results are similar to the above discussion except for higher ICE due to lack of oxygen-containing groups. Therefore, these experimental results are in match with the “adsorption–intercalation” mechanism: the low-voltage-plateau region is related to the intercalation into graphitic-like nanodomains and the slop-voltage region corresponds to the sites of different the adsorption energies, including adsorption in nanopores and on defects.[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a></span>]</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802694-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/261386c3-da6b-426d-9737-0152eb5778c2/smll201802694-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/261386c3-da6b-426d-9737-0152eb5778c2/smll201802694-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f1d17284-9ea0-4ed1-a9c6-97528538f097/smll201802694-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/261386c3-da6b-426d-9737-0152eb5778c2/smll201802694-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802694-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802694" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the HCs electrodes. a) The initial galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the hard carbon electrodes at a current rate of 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Summary of the capacity distribution during the charge and discharge process at a current rate of 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) analysis of the HC and the HC-4h discharged to 0 V. d) The cyclic voltammogram (CV) curves of the HC and HC-4h electrodes during the first cycle from 2.0 to 0 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of the HC and HC-4h electrodes discharged to 0 V were performed in Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0003">3</a>c. Both electrochemical impedance spectra show similar curves with two semicircles and a slope line. The two semicircles represent the resistance of SEI (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) and the charge transfer (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), respectively, while the slope line is ascribed to the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion process in hard carbon. According to an equivalent series resistance circuit inserted in Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0003">3</a>c, EIS curves are fitted, and the results are listed in Table S1 of the Supporting Information. The fitted impedance spectra showed that the resistance of SEI increased from 160.2 Ω for the HC to 210.5 Ω for the HC-4h, while the Rct increased as well, from 133.9 Ω for HC to 149.8 Ω for the HC-4h. It indicates that hard carbons with more defects and micropores could induce the formation of SEI, which blocks the charge transfer simultaneously, resulting in larger irreversible capacity and lower Coulombic efficiency in the initial cycle.</p>
         
         <p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) is an important way to elucidate the electrochemical properties. Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0003">3</a>d exhibits the CV curves of the HC and HC-4h electrodes with the same mass during the first cycle in the voltage range between 0 and 2 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. One pair of sharp peaks at around 0.1 V and a broad reduction peak at around 0.5 V were appeared in each the CV curve, agreeing with the initial galvanostatic charge–discharge curves. It is obviously found that the area of the broad reduction peak of the HC-4h electrode is larger than that of the HC electrode, while the peaks at around 0.1 V of the HC-4h electrode is smaller than those of the HC electrode. It indicates that the HC-4h causes more SEI film and exhibits less plateau capacity compared with the HC. It is consistent with the above galvanostatic charge–discharge experimental results (Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0003">3</a>a).</p>
         
         <p>It is well-known that in lithium-ion batteries, the reaction mechanism of the graphite anode is lithium-ion insertion between the graphite layers, exhibiting a potential plateau. If the reaction mechanism at low-potential plateau for sodium-ion insertion into hard carbon is similar to lithium-ion insertion into graphite, the electrochemical behaviors of the ball-milled graphite electrodes should also exhibit decreasing plateau capacity. For comparison, we use graphite as raw material for ball milling to explore the change in capacity for lithium-ion insertion into graphite. The ball-milling time for the graphite materials is 1 h and 4 h noted as graphite-1h and graphite-4h, respectively. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0004">4</a>a shows the XRD) patterns of the untreated graphite, graphite-1h, and graphite-4h. The graphite powers exhibit a strong peak at around 26.4°, which is regarded as the (002) peak. The (002) peak position does not change with ball-milling time, implying that the interlayer <i>d</i>-spacing (0.337 nm) does not change. With the Debye–Scherrer equation (Equation <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll201802694-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>), the average thickness of the graphitic domain (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>) could be calculated as 26.4 nm for graphite, 24.0 nm for graphite-1h and 22.2 nm for graphite-4h, respectively. With increasing ball-milling time, the average thickness of the graphitic domain (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>) decreases, which is similar to that of the hard carbon materials. The galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the graphite electrodes at a current rate of 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> were shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0004">4</a>b. It is found that with longer ball-milling time, there is a decreasing charge capacity along with the potential plateau. This phenomenon is due to the order of the graphite domain breakdown and the formation of disordered/amorphous carbon according to the previous reports.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0022_R_d145790702e858" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Electroanal. Chem. J. Power Sources J. Electrochem. Soc. J. Power Sources">22</a></span> It is obviously seen that the electrochemical performance of the ball-milled graphite electrode is also similar to that happened in the ball-milled hard carbon material (Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0003">3</a>a), indicating that the mechanism of sodium-ion insertion into hard carbon at low-potential-plateau region is similar to that of lithium-ion insertion into graphite: lithium ions insert into between the graphite layers to form graphite intercalation on compounds. The comparison experiments further gave powerful evidences for the “adsorption–insertion” mechanism of sodium-ion storage in hard carbon.[<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0003_R_d145790702e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater. Nat. Mater. Nano Lett. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿ ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Acta Phys. - Chim. Sin.">3</a>, <a href="#smll201802694-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0011_R_d145790702e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></span>] This is also consistent with the conclusions given by recent studies: the integrity of carbon layer in hard carbon has a significant impact on the potential-plateau capacity of sodium-ion storage.<span><a href="#smll201802694-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802694-bib-0014_R_d145790702e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802694-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7c57cc83-6443-494a-afa2-ae22a15830aa/smll201802694-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7c57cc83-6443-494a-afa2-ae22a15830aa/smll201802694-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1bceb741-88a8-43cf-98e7-b063a2cd655c/smll201802694-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7c57cc83-6443-494a-afa2-ae22a15830aa/smll201802694-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802694-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802694" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns and b) initial galvanostatic curves of the graphite, graphite-1h, and graphite-4h electrodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>According to the “adsorption–insertion” mechanism and above discussions, the sodiation behaviors of hard carbons with and without ball milling are schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0005">5</a>. The hard carbon materials have a turbostratic structure with layer-stacked domains. During sodiation, sodium ions adsorb first on the active and defect sites of the material surface (including pore surface), which depends on different potential, causing a potential slope (Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0005">5</a>a). If the surface of hard carbon presents some active functional groups containing O, N, P, S elements, the functional groups also can occur sodiation reaction at slope-potential region. Meanwhile, in initial sodiation process, the decomposition of the electrolyte and the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film also happen at this potential region (0.1–2 V), causing loss of the reversible capacity and decrease of the ICE. Then, sodium ions insert into the carbon layer of hard carbon, like intercalation of lithium ions into the graphite electrode (Figure <a href="#smll201802694-fig-0005">5</a>a). This process is a two-phase reaction, exhibiting a potential plateau. After ball milling, the hard carbon materials become smaller domains, which include more surface defects and disorder carbon layer structure. Moreover, the average width and thickness of the graphitic-like nanodomain decrease along with the number of the stacked graphene layers, so that fewer sodium ions intercalate into the graphene layers. Longer ball-milling time, less low-potential-plateau capacity. Moreover, more the surface defect sites cause more SEI film and lower ICE.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802694-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/88d6cf5c-954d-4524-a290-ce88f732ad96/smll201802694-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/88d6cf5c-954d-4524-a290-ce88f732ad96/smll201802694-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4f5b18f3-9409-4848-928f-900990c25bba/smll201802694-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/88d6cf5c-954d-4524-a290-ce88f732ad96/smll201802694-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802694-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802694" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the sodiation process of the HC and ball-milled HC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>According to the intrinsic relationship between the electrochemical performance and the microstructure of hard carbon materials, the hard carbon material with low specific surface area, low defect sites and proper interlayer spacing is an ideal choice for high-performance anodes in practical sodium-ion batteries. With a more clear understanding towards to the sodium mechanism in hard carbon, it is likely to synthesis such kind of hard carbon materials economically and environmentally, which is promising to achieve high-performance practical sodium-ion batteries for large-scale energy storage in the future.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802694-sec-0030">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802694-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we demonstrated the effect of the microstructure on the electrochemical performance of the hard carbon anode treated through simple ball-milling method. With increasing the ball-milling time, the hard carbon exhibited smaller and thinner graphitic-like nanodomain, larger specific surface area and micropore volume and more disorder structure. The change in the structure directly corresponds to sodium storage performance, for example, less plateau capacity and ICE with increasing ball-milling time. The experimental results are in match with the “adsorption–insertion” mechanism, which means that the slop-potential region is ascribed to the adsorption of sodium ions on the active and defect sites of the material surface, while the low-potential plateau is related to the intercalation into the graphene layers, like lithium-ion insertion into graphite. This work gives a new view from “up to down” preparation by simple ball-milling method to construct different microstructure hard carbon materials, which further understand the sodium storage mechanism of hard carbon. This work further boosts the development of high-performance hard carbon anode for the practical sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802694-sec-0040">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802694-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Material Preparation and Characterization</i>: The cellulose precursor (α-Cellulose, Aladdin Co.) was firstly pyrolyzed in 350 °C for 3 h with a heating rate of 2 °C min<sup>−1</sup>, then heated up to 1300 °C with a heating rate of 1 °C min<sup>−1</sup> in Ar atmosphere. After carbonization, the pyrolyzed HC was ball-milled by planetary ball mill (XGB2, Nanjing Bo Yun Tong Instrument Technology Co., Ltd, Nanjing, China) under air atmosphere for 1 h, 2 h, and 4 h, which were denoted as HC-1h, HC-2h, and HC-4h, respectively. The rotation speed is 500 rpm, and the weight ratio of powder materials to milling balls is set as 1 to 20. For further comparison, the graphite powder (FSN-1, Shanghai Shanshan Tech Co., Ltd) was also ball-milled for 1 and 4 h, respectively, in the same experimental condition.</p>
         
         <p>The morphologies of carbon materials were measured by SEM (ULTRA/PLUS, ZEISS). The crystalline structure of hard carbon materials was characterized by XRD (Bruker D8 Advance). Raman spectra were carried out by Raman spectroscopy (Renishaw in Via, Renishaw, 532 nm excitation wavelength). The pore distribution and specific surface area were measured by nitrogen adsorption–desorption measurements (ASAP 2020, Micromeritics, USA). The specific surface area was calculated by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller method. The mesopore volume and average pore size were carried out by Barrett–Joyner–Halenda model, while the micropore volume and average pore size were carried out by the Horvath–Kawazoe (H–K) model. XPS was measured by X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (ESCALAB250Xi, Al Kα excitation, 1486.6 eV).</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: Hard carbon electrodes were prepared by mixing 80 wt% hard carbon materials, 10 wt% Super P, 10 wt% polyacrylic acid (25 wt%) to form the slurry. The mixed slurry was coated onto a Cu foil and dried at 100 °C overnight under vacuum circumstance. The charge–discharge profiles were carried out by a 2016 coin cell by using hard carbon electrode as working electrode and a Na-disk as the counter electrode. The electrolyte used in the coin cell was mixed by ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1:1 vol) with 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub>. A Celgard 2400 microporous membrane was used as a separator. The graphite electrodes were prepared in the same way except that the binder was carboxymethyl (3 wt%). The charge–discharge profiles were conducted by a 2016 coin cell using graphite electrode as working electrode, a Li-disk as the counter electrode, 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in EC–DMC–ethyl methyl carbonate (1:1:1 vol) as the electrolyte, and a Celgard 2400 microporous membrane used as a separator. The loading mass of the HC anode is about 2 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. All the coin cells were collected in the glove box with water/oxygen content less than 1 ppm and tested at room temperature. The charge–discharge tests were measured by a Land cycler (Wuhan Kingnuo Electronic Co., China). CV curves of coin cells were carried out by Gamry (Reference 3000, USA). To eliminate artificial error for defining the slope and the plateau capacity, the slope line and the plateau line in the discharge curve of HC electrode were prolonged to obtain an intersection, which divides the whole capacity into slope capacity and plateau capacity. The EIS were conducted by a modular potentiostats/galvanostats instruments (Metrohm Autolab B.V. PGSTAT128N) in the frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz with an AC signal amplitude of 10 mV.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802694-sec-0050-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>

            <div class="accordion__content" style="display: none;" role="region" aria-labelledby="references-section-1" id="smll201802694-bibl-0001">

                  <li data-bib-id="smll201802694-bib-0001"><span class="bullet">1</span>a) <span class="author">M. D. Slater</span>, <span class="author">D. Kim</span>, <span class="author">E.
